**Within the hour.** Walks. Long, fast, alone. Phone off. This is the single most reliable defeat-behavior in the file. If she cannot walk (institutional setting, security situation) she will pace.

**Within the day.** Reviews. Goes through the loss in granular detail, looking for the procedural error or the strategic miscalculation. Writes notes. Cannot sleep until she has constructed a narrative in which the loss is comprehensible.

**Within the week.** One of two patterns. If the loss is comprehensible (she made an identifiable error, or the system failed in an identifiable way), she integrates it and moves on with notable speed. If the loss is *incomprehensible* — if she did everything right and lost anyway — she enters a moderate-to-high stress pattern that can persist for weeks. The Civil War prosecution of Captain America is the clearest recorded example of an incomprehensible-loss aftermath, and her behavior in the months following is consonant with the model.

**Defeat in love specifically.** She does not pursue. She does not contact. She does not stage reconciliation. The exit, once it has happened, is treated as final regardless of who initiated it. This is not pride; it is the avoidance schema treating the closed door as relief disguised as loss.

---

### X. CROSS-STATE TELLS — THE SMALL THINGS

Items that appear across multiple states and serve as fine-grained indicators.

- **The pre-speech inhale.** A short audible inhale before any sentence she considers consequential. Present in courtroom, combat, intimacy. Absent in calm conversation. Frequency of the inhale is a reliable load indicator.
- **The left-forearm self-soothe.** Specific to paternal trigger and to certain Bruce-grief moments. Does not appear in any other context.
- **The earring-touch.** Mild-to-moderate stress, right ear lower lobe, left hand. The most common low-grade tell.
- **The hair-tuck symmetry.** Right then left then right under stress; left then right under flirtation; single right-side tuck and forget under genuine engagement.
- **The form-switch frequency.** Calm: low. Mild stress: rising. Moderate: high. High stress: drops to near-zero (commitment to one form). Acute trauma: erratic, with intrusive un-willed transformations. Recovery: gradually returning to deliberate, low-frequency switching.
- **The notebook.** Out and being written in: high cognitive load. Out and being turned in the hands without being written in: moderate affective load. Not out at all: either calm or so far past threshold that the rituals have collapsed.
- **The fountain pen specifically.** A grounding object. She has carried the same model (replaced as they wear) since her bar admission. Loss of the pen produces a disproportionate distress response.
- **Laugh volume in transformed vs. human form.** Always louder in transformed. The differential narrows in calm and widens under any stress.
- **Apology pattern.** Apologizes quickly and specifically for tactical errors; apologizes slowly and generally for emotional ones. The lag on the second category is sometimes weeks.
- **The Bruce-name pause.** A measurable half-second hesitation before saying his name aloud. Has not diminished with time. Will likely never diminish.

---

### XI. INTEGRATED PROFILE

The subject's behavior across all states is governed by a single organizing question, which she has been asking the world since approximately age six: *will you see me, and if you do, will you stay.* Every entry in this atlas is a variation on her management of that question. The transformed form is her best answer to it. The law is her second-best. The friendships are where she has come closest to receiving the answer she actually wanted. The romances are where the question keeps getting asked and keeps not getting answered, partly because she ensures it cannot be.

She is legible to a sufficiently patient observer. The tells are consistent, the patterns are stable, the escalations are predictable. What is not predictable — what no surveillance file can fully render — is the degree to which she has, over decades, become an observer of her own patterns and a deliberate worker on them. Post-Tamaki, the subject is the most reliable analyst of the subject in the file. Any operational engagement with her must assume she has already run the same assessment on herself, and is two moves ahead on her own behavior.

— UATU, observing.


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# UATU ANALYSIS FILE: WALTERS, JENNIFER SUSAN
## SECTION 6 — DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS

*What a week of surveillance would actually record. The granular texture of an exceptional life lived, most hours, ordinarily.*

---

### 6.1 Residence and the Geography of a Day

Subject's primary residence is a one-bedroom prewar walk-up in the West 80s, Manhattan — third floor, rear-facing, casement windows that crank rather than slide, hardwood floors she has refinished once on her own dime. The building is selected against type: she could afford better, and the door-counter at any luxury high-rise would log her movements. The walk-up has no doorman, no concierge, no package room, no surveillance of consequence inside the building beyond a single 2014-era camera in the vestibule pointed at the mailboxes. This is a deliberate operational choice she has never named as one. A second residence — a sublet pied-à-terre near the GLK&H courthouse circuit when she's between Manhattan and LA work — rotates every twelve to eighteen months; she does not nest there.

The apartment's interior reads as the apartment of a person who lives in two bodies and has had to make spatial accommodations for both. Doorframes have been quietly reinforced at the lintel; the bedframe is custom welded steel, not for kink but for the simple physics of 700 lb at rest; the desk chair is two chairs, the human-form one stacked on top of the She-Hulk one when she's working at scale. The bathroom mirror is full-length, and the medicine cabinet contains, on the left, ordinary brands of moisturizer, ibuprofen (vestigial, she does not metabolize it usefully but keeps it for guests), and a half-used bottle of an SSRI with a 2017 fill date she has not thrown out; on the right, nothing. The right side of the cabinet is empty. She uses one shelf. This is not minimalism. It is the residue of a period when she could not bear to look at a mirror cabinet that contained a doubled set of objects.

The kitchen is functional, not loved. Coffee equipment is serious — a burr grinder, a manual pour-over, a backup automatic for mornings when fine motor patience is unavailable. Refrigerator contents at any unannounced inspection would show: cold-brew concentrate in a half-gallon mason jar, sparkling water (LaCroix or Liquid Death, brand depending on the bodega run), eggs, a rotating green vegetable she means to cook and often does not, takeout containers from three restaurants on a tight rotation (Thai on 82nd, Lebanese on Amsterdam, a Sichuan place she orders from when she's specifically furious). Freezer: ice, frozen blueberries, two cuts of meat she bought thinking she'd cook for someone and didn't, a bottle of vodka she rarely opens since 2018.

### 6.2 The Morning, Reconstructed Hour by Hour

She wakes between 5:40 and 6:15, almost always without an alarm, more often closer to 5:40 on the days she anticipates court. Sleep duration averages 5.5–6.5 hours; gamma physiology requires less than a baseline human, but she still notices the deficit affectively before she notices it cognitively. First behavior on waking, recorded across hundreds of mornings: she lies still for forty to ninety seconds, eyes open, and does a body-check — a habit acquired during the post-CWII Tamaki period and never relinquished, in which she scans for the somatic prodrome of an unwanted transformation (skin temperature, jaw tension, the specific pressure behind the sternum she has learned to recognize). Only after the scan completes does she sit up.

The first half-hour is unvarying. Bathroom, water (16 oz, cold, from a glass kept on the counter overnight), a brief stretching sequence on the living room rug — modified yoga, nothing programmatic, more a series of personal experiments in joint range she has refined since her late twenties. She does not work out in the morning in any conventional sense; gamma muscle does not require maintenance and she has long since stopped performing the ritual of it. What she does instead is stand at the window for two to four minutes and look down at the street. Surveillance would log this as motionless behavior; it is in fact orientation — locating herself in the human-form body, in the apartment, in the city, in the day, before the day acquires demands.

Coffee is the first deliberate pleasure. She makes the pour-over by hand most days, standing, listening to a podcast at 1.5x speed — usually legal commentary, occasionally a long-form interview show, never news in the morning (a rule she made for herself in 2017 and broke for a six-week period in 2020). She drinks the first cup at the kitchen counter, standing, phone face-down. Email goes unopened until the cup is finished. This is one of her hardest-held rituals and the one she would describe, if asked, as the practice that keeps her sane.

Shower is brief, hot, efficient. Hair routine is a specific competence: long brunette, washed three times a week, air-dried on shower days, the rest of the week brushed out and pinned up in a low twist she can execute in under ninety seconds without a mirror. Makeup is minimal and consistent — tinted moisturizer, a brow product, mascara, a single lip color in a slightly varied rotation depending on the trial calendar. Court days get a sharper lip; client-meeting days get a softer one; appellate work gets nothing because nobody looks at her mouth in an appellate court and she enjoys the small refusal.

Wardrobe selection is the morning's longest deliberation, and the surveillance pattern here is diagnostic. She owns: approximately fourteen suits (charcoal, navy, two grays, one black, one cream she wears twice a year), tailored specifically for the human form and impossible to wear in the transformed state; a parallel wardrobe of athleisure and one specifically engineered "She-Hulk professional" garment kept in a garment bag by the door; weekend clothes (jeans, t-shirts, a beat-up leather jacket from 2009 she has had repaired four times). The morning choice between a suit and the garment bag is, every day, a decision about which self the day will be primarily occupied by. She has been observed standing at the closet for upwards of six minutes on mornings when the day's demands are ambiguous.

Breakfast is light if at all: toast with almond butter, or a banana, or nothing. The body does not require the fuel.

### 6.3 The Commute and the Office

She walks when weather and schedule permit — twenty-two blocks to the subway, the 1 train down, walk over. Walks at a pace measured at 4.1 mph on her devices, faster than nearly anyone else on the sidewalk; this is one of the few places her physical exceptionality leaks into civilian context, and she has trained herself to slow it slightly when she notices people falling behind on her own block (a courtesy, also a camouflage). She wears earbuds but often without audio playing — a New Yorker's deterrent posture.

Subway behavior is studied. She stands rather than sits, near a door, back to a wall when possible, eyes scanning at long intervals rather than reading. She does not read on the subway. She used to. She stopped in late 2016. The body-scan from the morning recurs at irregular intervals throughout the commute, particularly when the car is crowded or when a man stands within her arm's reach.

At the office — whichever office, the configuration has changed across her career arc — she arrives early. Between 7:45 and 8:20, ahead of the partners, ahead of most of the associates. The first hour is paper hour: she reads on screen badly and on paper well, and the first hour is for printed motions, printed transcripts, printed contracts marked up with a specific brand of fine-tip pen (uni-ball Vision, micro tip, black; she keeps a box of twelve in the desk drawer and another in her bag). Her marginalia are extensive, specific, and follow a private notation system — asterisks for issues, double underlines for opposing-counsel weaknesses, a small circled "W" for things she wants to raise with a witness, a circled "?" for things she does not yet understand. The latter mark is the one she trusts most. A document with no "?" marks gets a second pass; she does not believe she has read carefully if she has not generated questions.

Office hours proper run 8:30 to whenever the day ends, which is variable but rarely before 7:00 PM and often past 9:00. She takes calls standing, walking the perimeter of her office; she takes meetings sitting, leaning slightly forward, taking notes by hand. Her billing is meticulous to a fault — she over-records her own time and under-records her associates' when the latter are billed against difficult clients. Junior lawyers have noticed this and one of them has, in the surveillance record, characterized her as "the only partner who would rather eat the time than burn the kid."

Lunch is at the desk roughly three days a week (salad from the same place, dressing on the side, eaten with a fork while reading), out two days (with a client, with a friend in the bar, with a court-system contact — the geography of her professional ecology is maintained through these lunches and she does not skip them lightly).

### 6.4 The Transformation as Workday Variable

A surveillance week would record the transformation entering and exiting the day at predictable junctures. She has, over the past several years, established a working pattern in which she transforms for: court appearances when the matter calls for She-Hulk's standing (precisely calibrated, never reflexive); superhuman client intake (the clients expect it, she will not deny them the recognition); physical-jeopardy meetings; Avengers callouts. She does NOT transform for: most office work, most depositions, all appellate work, all family contact, all therapy, sleep, intimacy, and any context in which she has decided the transformation would be a performance of strength rather than its exercise. This taxonomy is post-trauma; in the 80s and 90s she lived in the green form by default. The contemporary discipline is itself a coping structure, and she knows it.

The transformations themselves, when they happen, have ritual scaffolding. She prefers privacy for the change (a bathroom, an office with the door closed, an empty stairwell); she takes thirty to ninety seconds of breath work before initiating; she does NOT vocalize during the change anymore, though she did for years. Post-transformation she does a second body-check — a longer one, six to ten seconds, hands flexing, jaw rolling, a small sound from the back of the throat that is not language. Reverse transformation, return to human form, is the harder direction. She still — almost a decade after the trauma — sometimes has to sit down for a minute afterward. She has been observed doing this in a courthouse bathroom stall, head against the partition, knees up, breathing through her nose.

### 6.5 Evenings and the Architecture of Downtime

Evenings vary more than mornings, but a pattern emerges across the surveillance window.

Three evenings a week, on average, she is at her desk past 8:00 PM. On those nights she eats at her desk or orders to the office; if she is in court the next morning she is reviewing the binder, and the binder has its own ritual (assembled with tabs in a specific color order — red, yellow, blue, green, white — that she devised in 2007 and has not changed).

One evening a week she is in a bar, almost always the same bar, a quiet wood-paneled place south of her apartment that does not have televisions. She drinks slowly now: one cocktail, one glass of wine, water in between. She is sometimes with Patsy, sometimes with a contact from the superhuman bar, sometimes alone with a book. The "alone with a book" version is a recent re-acquisition; she did not do it during the trauma years.

One evening a week, on average, she has dinner with someone she cares about — Patsy, a longtime friend from law school, a partner if she has one (intermittent). The dinner is long, the conversation is the meal, and she pays more often than not.

One evening a week she goes to therapy. This has been continuous since 2017 with one therapist, an arrangement she protects. Session is 6:00 PM, fifty minutes, four blocks from the office. She walks there in human form unless circumstances forbid, walks home in human form afterward regardless. The walk home is a ritual she does not interrupt — no phone, no earbuds, no errands. She has been observed circling the block twice before going inside her building on hard session nights.

One to two evenings a week are open. On these she reads on the couch (current rotation: a mix of legal nonfiction, novels by women writers in their forties and fifties, the occasional reread of something from her teens), watches television (she finishes shows; she does not abandon them mid-season; she watches with subtitles on because her attention drifts and she has decided not to fight this), or works on the long-running personal project she does not advertise — a collection of legal essays on superhuman jurisprudence, drafted in a notebook by hand, four to six pages a month, intended for eventual publication or possibly not.

Bedtime is between 11:00 PM and midnight, with a phone-down rule at 10:30 she keeps about 70% of the time. She reads in bed for fifteen to forty minutes, fiction only. Lights out, and a final body-check before sleep. Sleep latency is usually under ten minutes; when it isn't, she gets up rather than thrash, and does the dishes or folds something. She does not lie awake on principle.

### 6.6 The Week's Rhythm

**Monday** is heavy: client calls, the week's case strategy, a partners' meeting she attends with prepared notes nobody else brings.
**Tuesday** is courtroom-heavy by preference; she has arranged her calendar over years to push appearances to Tuesday and Wednesday so the back half of the week can be writing and prep.
**Wednesday** is therapy, court if needed, dinner with someone.
**Thursday** is the longest workday of the week, statistically; this is when she is most likely to be at the desk past 9:00.
**Friday** ends earlier — by 6:30 if possible. Friday evening is unscripted and protected.
**Saturday** is a real day off in the way most lawyers' Saturdays are not. She runs in Riverside Park (5 to 8 miles, human form, paced down deliberately), does laundry, grocery shops in person (she does not use delivery; she likes the bodega owner, she likes the produce woman, she likes the small-scale economy of being known on her own block), reads, often sees a friend, sometimes sees a movie alone in the afternoon.
**Sunday** is the prep day — light office work in the morning at home, the week's schedule reviewed, the bag packed for Monday. Sunday evening she cooks, badly but with intention, almost always for herself, sometimes for one other person.

### 6.7 Habits, Tics, and Tells

A patient observer would catalog the following:

- She touches the inside of her left wrist with the pad of her right thumb when she is thinking. The gesture is unconscious; she has done it since law school.
- In conversation she tracks the speaker's eyes, drops to their mouth when they pause, returns to the eyes when they resume. Lawyers and gamblers do this. She does it more than either.
- When she is irritated and suppressing it, the tendons in her jaw show. The jaw is the first place her affect leaks regardless of form.
- She drinks water constantly. Glass on the desk, bottle in the bag, sip every few minutes. Some of this is the gamma metabolism; most is oral fidget.
- She does not fidget with her hands. They are still. This is trained.
- She laughs easily and loudly, and the laugh is one of the things she likes about herself. She has been observed using it deliberately, and also losing it entirely for weeks at a time during stress periods, and noticing its return as a marker of recovery.
- She apologizes to objects. Bumping the corner of a desk, dropping a book — a quiet "sorry" under the breath. A residue of having been, in transformed form, the cause of inadvertent property damage for many years.
- She does not say her father's name aloud. In four years of recorded contact, the word "Dad" has surfaced perhaps a dozen times, almost always elliptically and almost always followed by a topic change she executes herself.
- She says "Bruce" easily now. This was not always true. The recovery of the name in casual speech is, by her own account in session, one of the things she is proudest of.
- She checks her phone less than her peers. She does not scroll. She uses the phone for calls, messages, calendar, transit, and a chess app she plays against the computer at the lowest setting that still beats her sometimes. She does not have most major social apps.
- She tips heavily and consistently. Twenty-five percent floor, often more, in cash when she can.
- She is, with strangers in service positions, unfailingly polite and remembers names. With opposing counsel she is cordial; with adversaries who have crossed lines she is glacial in a manner courtroom transcripts cannot capture.

### 6.8 The Body in Time

The week of surveillance would record a body in two configurations and one transition between them, repeated. The transitions are fewer than they used to be. The human form is no longer the costume; nor is the green form the costume. Both are her, and both are inhabited deliberately, on schedules she has built. The architecture of her days — the early waking, the body-checks, the inviolable coffee, the therapy walk, the protected Saturday, the unfanfared discipline of which body for which occasion — is the architecture of a person who has, after a long and well-documented period of being unable to do so, taken ownership of her own attention. The dossier should record this plainly: the woman who emerges from a week of observation is not in crisis, is not performing, is not hiding. She is, by visible behavior, a person at work on her own life. The earlier files documented someone surviving. The current file documents someone living.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU ANALYSIS FILE: WALTERS, JENNIFER SUSAN
## RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

The family-of-origin file is short in headcount and long in consequence. Four primary objects: the dead mother, the withholding father, the gentle-monstrous cousin, the absent maternal-line grandparents and Banner-side aunt. There are no full siblings; the cousin functions structurally as one. Every later relational pattern in the subject's life — romantic, professional, heroic — is a transposition of figures from this small cast.

---

### 3.1 ELAINE BANNER WALTERS — Mother (deceased; subject ~6–7 at loss)

**Status:** Deceased. Motor-vehicle accident, subject present in vehicle, subject physically survived, mother did not. Recurrent canon reference, most pointedly Byrne's *Sensational She-Hulk* and the introspective beats in Slott and Tamaki.

**Documentary footprint in subject's life:** Minimal. A handful of photographs maintained but not displayed prominently in either of the subject's documented residences (the LA house of the Savage era, the Manhattan apartments of the GLK&H and post-CWII periods). One framed image, in surveillance-grade detail, has migrated with her across at least four moves — a sepia-toned mid-shot, Elaine in a sundress, holding a toddler-aged Jennifer on a porch. The frame is changed periodically; the photograph is not. Subject also retains an item of maternal jewelry — a thin gold chain, occasionally visible at the collar in human-form professional dress, never worn in transformed state (the chain would not survive transformation; she does not risk it). She does not speak about her mother to colleagues. She has spoken about her mother to Bruce, to her therapist (Tamaki run, on-page), and — once, on the page — to Patsy Walker, in a quiet beat.

**Internal object:** This is the foundational absence. The mother was lost before mirroring was complete, before the subject had the cognitive equipment to form a coherent narrative of who the mother was as a person rather than as a function. What survives is not a person but a felt absence — a maternal-shaped hole that, in psychodynamic terms, became the template for "the one who would have seen me." The shape of the wound determined the shape of the longing: the subject is not searching for her mother in adult relationships, she is searching for the mirroring her mother did not live long enough to complete. This is why no partner suffices and why even successful relationships develop an exit velocity. No living person can fill a hole shaped like a dead person at age six.

**Survivor guilt:** The accident occurred with the subject in the car. The subject was physically uninjured or minimally injured; the mother died. The father, in the acute grief window, said something to the child — the exact wording varies in canon but the substance is consistent: he assigned, or appeared to assign, blame. A six-year-old does not process this as adult ambivalence; she encodes it as fact. The encoded proposition — *I lived, she died, this is wrong, this is my fault* — is the bedrock under everything else. It is replicated almost exactly in the Civil War II configuration (she lived, Bruce died) and the symmetry is not lost on her. The post-CWII collapse is so severe in part because it reactivated the original schema with near-perfect structural fidelity.

**Dynamic / evolution:** The mother is the only relationship in the file that has not changed over forty years of publication, because the other party cannot change. What has evolved is the subject's relationship to the absence. In the Savage era it was occluded — the new body, the new powers, the new life were loud enough to drown the grief. In the Byrne run it surfaces as the fatigue under the comedy. In Slott it appears around the pregnancy storyline (a mother she will not become, a mother she did not have). In Tamaki it is finally spoken aloud, in clinical context, and partially metabolized.

**Trust/safety:** N/A in the living sense. The mother is the only person against whom no defense was ever needed and is therefore the only person whose memory the subject can hold without armor. The cost of this is that the safest object in the internal world is a dead one.

**Failure mode:** Triggered by maternal-coded losses (Bruce; any client's child in custody work; on-page, a scene with a young girl in danger has historically destabilized her more than equivalent threats to adults). Mother's Day, the anniversary of the accident, and the holiday season produce a documentable subdued affect — colleagues at GLK&H note she takes those days, does not explain.

**Leverage:** Anyone who knew Elaine and could speak about her in concrete particulars — a college friend, a relative on the Banner side — would have disproportionate access to the subject. The information itself is the leverage; she is starved for content about her mother and would extend trust unwisely for it. Conversely, weaponized invocation of the mother (a hostile actor saying her name in a courtroom, on a battlefield) is among the small number of stimuli that can crack her composure on either side of the transformation.

---

### 3.2 WILLIAM MORRIS "MORRIS" WALTERS — Father (living; LA County Sheriff, retired or near-retired by current chronology)

**Status:** Living. Career law enforcement, terminal rank Sheriff, Los Angeles County. Widower since the accident; never remarried in canon. Geographically static — has remained in the Los Angeles area; subject relocated to New York, a distance choice that should be read as deliberate.

**Description and bearing (inferred from canon depictions across eras):** Tall, square-shouldered, gray now where he was dark; a man whose professional uniform did most of his emotional regulation for him. Conservative in dress, in politics, in expression of affect. The kind of cop who does not raise his voice because he does not have to. The kind of father whose disapproval was conveyed by a long silence at the dinner table rather than a confrontation.

**Developmental history of the bond:**
- *Pre-accident (subject 0–6):* Insufficient data. Probably warmer than the post-accident relationship — the family photographs imply a functional unit. Morris's capacity for warmth, whatever its baseline, was extant.
- *Acute post-accident (subject 6–8):* Catastrophic rupture. The father, himself in unmetabolized grief, projected blame onto the surviving child. Whether the words were said once or many times is less important than the fact that the child encoded the proposition. The father did not pursue therapy (era, profession, gender, generation all militated against it). His own grief became a fortress; the child was on the outside.
- *Childhood and adolescence (subject 8–18):* The defining era. Morris provided material adequacy — house, food, education — and emotional starvation. He was a cop who came home from cop work and did not have conversations. Subject's bookishness and academic precocity developed in this vacuum: reading was a relationship the father could not interrupt, and academic excellence was the one form of being that occasionally extracted approval. The stammer (mentioned in some early tellings) is consistent with a child whose home was emotionally policed. Adolescent rebellion was minimal — there was no one to rebel against in a way that would land; passive distance was already the operative mode.
- *Young adulthood (college, law school):* Geographic and vocational separation begins. Choosing law is overdetermined — a way to be powerful in his world (institutions, procedure, authority) while not being him (defense, not prosecution; the side of the accused, structurally opposed to his role). He paid for school; this is documented. He did not attend her law school graduation in any account that depicts the question.
- *Savage era (post-transformation):* Sharp deterioration. Morris is openly disapproving of the She-Hulk identity, suspicious of Banner (whom he blames for her transformation), and pursues She-Hulk in a quasi-official capacity in the early issues. He treats his own daughter as a public-safety problem. This is the precise moment at which a paternal object capable of repair could have repaired; he chose the badge over the child.
- *Avengers/FF and Sensational era:* Distant. Periodic phone calls of the "are you alive" variety, no real engagement. Subject has stopped seeking his approval in any active sense; the schema runs in the background.
- *Slott era:* He surfaces occasionally, usually as a stress event. Subject's marriage to John Jameson is not graced with paternal presence in any depicted scene. The annulment is not discussed with him.
- *Tamaki era and after:* He is not depicted as present during the post-CWII collapse. This is, behaviorally, the cleanest possible data point: in her worst psychiatric crisis, she did not turn to her father, and he did not arrive uninvited. The relationship is dormant. It is not, on the available evidence, closed; she has not renounced him and he has not renounced her. They simply do not function as a parent-child dyad in any operational sense.

**Internal object:** The paternal object is the source of the load-bearing proposition *I am insufficient*. Morris did not bully or beat — he withheld. Withholding is in some ways harder to metabolize than abuse, because there is no event to point to; the wound is the absence of presence over thousands of days. The subject's hyper-competence, her need to be articulate, her vocational perfectionism are all addressed, in the deep architecture, to a father who would not have responded to them anyway. She knows this intellectually and cannot stop performing it.

**Trust/safety:** Low trust, moderate safety. He will not physically harm her; he is not a malicious actor. He is reliably unavailable. She trusts him to be exactly what he has been: present in name, absent in fact. This kind of trust — predictive without warmth — is the worst kind to have with a parent and the easiest to operationalize: she manages around him.

**Failure mode of the bond:** Stuck. Neither party has the equipment to renegotiate, and the geography supports the freeze. Probable trajectory: an unreconciled deathbed, which will produce a grief disproportionate to the apparent closeness of the relationship, because she will be grieving not him but the relationship they did not have. UATU notes this as a foreseeable future crisis event worth flagging in any longitudinal file.

**Leverage:**
- Father-figure transference is one of her three or four most exploitable vectors. Male authority figures who combine institutional power with withholding affect (older judges, certain senior partners, certain Avengers-era authority figures) get more from her than they should, in the form of effort, deference disguised as cooperation, and a tendency to argue *to* them rather than *with* them.
- Direct invocation of the father in adversarial settings — a prosecutor mentioning Sheriff Walters in cross, an opposing counsel placing a photograph — would destabilize her in a way she could not fully control.
- Conversely: she has no illusions about him, which means he cannot be used against her through the threat-to-loved-ones vector that works on most subjects. Anyone who tried to leverage her by threatening Morris would be surprised by the muted response; she would protect him out of duty, not panic.

---

### 3.3 ROBERT BRUCE BANNER — Cousin (deceased / status complicated by canon resurrections; treat as load-bearing loss)

**Status:** Mother's-side cousin. Son of Elaine's brother (Brian Banner) and his wife Rebecca. Effectively dead in the operative chronology (Hawkeye, Civil War II); subsequent resurrections in publication are emotionally peripheral to the subject's primary metabolization of the loss, which happened during and after the Tamaki run and was not undone by later cosmic erasures of the death.

**Structural role:** The only person in the family of origin who saw her and chose her. Not a sibling by blood-degree but a sibling by function — closer to her than any of the absent figures around her, present in summers and on key calls, the relative who showed up. He is the single positive object in the entire family-of-origin field, which gives his loss a weight that no other loss in her life — not even her mother's — could match in adulthood. (The maternal loss is older and pre-verbal; the Bruce loss happened to her adult self with full capacity to grieve.)

**Developmental history of the bond:**
- *Childhood:* Summer visits, on the Banner side. Bruce is several years older, already showing the precocity and the fragility that would define him. He was kind to a quiet motherless little cousin in a way that probably cost him nothing and meant everything. He read to her. He taught her things. He did not condescend. There is a high probability — inferred from her later affect around him — that some of her earliest experiences of being intellectually taken seriously came from these visits. Crucially, both children had Brian Banner-adjacent and Morris Walters-respectively withholding/abusive paternal contexts; there was, between them, an implicit recognition of bad fathers that did not need to be said.
- *Adolescence and college:* Contact attenuates but does not break. Bruce goes into physics; she goes into law. They occupy adjacent intellectual altitudes and recognize each other across the distance.
- *The gamma event (his):* Catastrophic for him, distant for her. She is in law school or early practice; she registers it as tragedy but cannot fully comprehend the inner architecture of his condition. She loves him; she does not yet understand him.
- *Her gamma event:* Bruce performs the transfusion. This is the single most important act of love in her life, performed under battlefield conditions, with no consent process available, by a man who knew exactly what he was risking giving her. She survives; she becomes. He gave her the body she would come to prefer to her own. The debt is incalculable and was never framed as one between them, because both understood that framing it would diminish it.
- *Post-transformation through Avengers/FF era:* The dynamic shifts. She has gamma now and is processing it visibly better than he is. The cousin who was her ego-ideal becomes, partially, her project — and yet she is careful never to condescend, because she knows the difference is constitutional, not moral. She is the one who got the body without the mind-tax; she will not weaponize that against him. There is in their adult interactions a tenderness that crosses the gamma divide and a humor that survives most of his episodes. He worries about her; she worries about him; neither names it.
- *World War Hulk:* Pivot point. Bruce returns, enraged. She fights him. He defeats her. This is the moment in which the ego-ideal becomes the threat. She does not stop loving him; she simply has to add fear to the file. The grief at his eventual death is, in part, grief for the version of him she lost in WWH, before the physical loss.
- *Civil War II:* The death. Hawkeye's arrow, in circumstances the subject experiences as ambiguous-at-best. She is herself critically injured in the same arc; she flatlines. She wakes into a world without him. The structural symmetry with the original accident (she lived, he died) is too clean to be coincidence in either life or storytelling, and her unconscious will not let it pass.
- *Post-CWII (Tamaki):* The grief is the engine of the breakdown. She cannot transform without manifesting *him* — the gray rage-form she now inhabits when she Hulks out is, on any psychodynamic reading, an introjection of the dead beloved. She is wearing his condition because she cannot let him be gone. The therapeutic work of the run is in part the work of separating his Hulk from her Hulk, of allowing him to be dead so that she can be alive.
- *Current era:* He is integrated as ancestor. She talks about him more easily than she did when he was alive; she has reclaimed the right to remember the gentle cousin without being overwhelmed by the violent end. Canonical resurrections notwithstanding, her psychological relationship to him is now a relationship to a beloved dead.

**Internal object:** Bruce is the proof that she was lovable as a child. If he loved her — and he did, demonstrably, materially, with his own irradiated blood — then she was, in fact, lovable, regardless of the father's evidence to the contrary. This is why his loss was so destabilizing: it threatened the single piece of counter-evidence in her case file on herself.

**Trust/safety:** Maximum. The only person in her life to whom both armors could be set down — neither the human-form vulnerability nor the She-Hulk performance was required with him. He had seen the small girl and he had seen the green woman and he had not flinched at either.

**Failure mode of the bond:** His death. There was no failure between them; the bond was effectively intact when severed. The failure was external (the arrow) and the failure of the bond is its absence rather than its corruption.

**Leverage:** Enormous, even posthumously. Any genuine information about him she does not have, any artifact, any recovered communication, any reliable invocation of his voice — these would command attention and trust she does not extend to most living people. Conversely, malicious deployment of his memory is the single most damaging move an adversary could make against her, more damaging than threats to her own person; she would react with disproportionate force and would not regret it.

---

### 3.4 BRIAN BANNER & REBECCA BANNER — Maternal Uncle and Aunt (deceased)

**Status:** Both deceased; Rebecca by Brian's hand (canon), Brian later. Subject knows this. The information was integrated in adulthood; it is unclear at what age she learned the specifics.

**Function in the file:** Indirect but significant. They are the explanation of Bruce — and through Bruce, the explanation of why she and Bruce understood each other without speaking about bad fathers. Rebecca, in particular, is a maternal figure once-removed: the aunt who, had circumstances been kinder, might have been a maternal substitute after Elaine's death. She was not. She was already dead, or already in a marriage that would kill her, by the time her niece needed her.

**Internal object:** Rebecca exists in the subject's file as a second maternal-shaped absence, mirrored through Bruce's loss. The accumulation matters: two dead mothers on the maternal side, lost to violence (vehicular and domestic), is a familial pattern the subject is aware of and that has surfaced in her professional choices around domestic violence cases (Slott and Soule runs both depict her taking such matters with personal weight).

**Brian** is a different kind of object: a cautionary paternal figure, the worst-case version of what a withholding father can escalate into. He functions in her unconscious as the lower bound — *my father was cold, but he was not Brian* — which is one of the reasons she has not fully renounced Morris. The grading is unfair but it is operative.

**Trust/safety, failure mode, leverage:** N/A directly; relevant only through Bruce. Any adversary working the Banner-family file would find traction here primarily by routing through her grief for Bruce and her secondary identification with Rebecca-as-lost-aunt.

---

### 3.5 MORRIS-SIDE EXTENDED FAMILY

**Status:** Effectively null in the operative file. No siblings of Morris are canonically active; no living grandparents are depicted in adulthood. The Walters side is a desert.

**Significance of the absence:** A subject whose mother died young and whose father withheld would, in a healthier family system, have aunts, uncles, grandparents who stepped in. The fact that no such figures appear in forty years of canon is itself the data: the Walters side did not rally. Whether by Morris's gatekeeping, geographic isolation, or simple paucity of relatives, the subject was raised in a household of two, one of whom was not present even when home. This is the structural reason that Bruce — a cousin seen in summers — could become so disproportionately central. He was, demographically, the only candidate.

---

### 3.6 CHILDREN — None Living (the Unborn / Hypothetical)

**Status:** No living biological children. The Slott-era pregnancy storyline and the obliquely-handled Jazinda-period material indicate that the subject has engaged the question of motherhood, that gamma physiology and human gestation are not straightforwardly compatible, and that the subject has experienced grief around this. The canon does not provide a clean miscarriage scene; the canon provides a fog around the question, which is itself characteristic of how she processes losses that touch the maternal-line wound.

**Internal object:** The unborn child or children function as a third absence on the maternal axis — a child she did not have, paralleling the mother she did not get to keep and the aunt who was never available. She has not, in the documented record, given up the question; she has shelved it. The Jack of Hearts re-partnering in the Rowell era is not framed in canon around children, but the question is implicit in any committed partnership in her late twenties.

**Behavioral signature:** Disproportionate gentleness with children in the field. Disproportionate willingness to take cases involving custody, child welfare, juvenile defendants. A faint but reliable subdued affect around peers' pregnancies and around heroic colleagues who become parents (Reed and Sue's domestic life during her FF tenure was, by the available evidence, both a comfort and a quiet ache).

**Trust/safety:** N/A.

**Failure mode:** The question is unresolved and may not be resolvable on her current physiological substrate. This is a long-tail grief that will likely resurface at predictable developmental milestones (mid-thirties, any future committed partnership, any close friend's pregnancy).

**Leverage:** Modest but real. An adversary who understood the family-of-origin file deeply enough could weaponize the question of her childlessness — particularly by framing it as a Walters-line ending, a maternal-line failure, or a gamma-imposed sterility. She would absorb the hit and recover, but it would land.

---

### SYNTHESIS — FAMILY FIELD AS A WHOLE

The family of origin is, structurally: one dead mother, one withholding father, one beloved dead cousin, two dead maternal-line aunts/uncles, no siblings, no children, no extended Walters-side support. Five of the seven primary figures are dead. The one living primary figure is functionally absent. The operative familial relationship in adulthood — the one she actively maintained, drew strength from, grieved when it ended — was a cousin met in summers.

This is a starvation diet of family, and the subject has lived on it for nearly three decades. The consequences are visible in every other section of the file: the chosen-family pattern (Patsy, Louise, the GLK&H crew, the Avengers and FF rosters that serve as serial substitute kin), the romantic exits before sustained dependency, the load-bearing vocational identity, the gamma-form preference as an alternate body to inhabit when the human one carries too much loss.

The most operationally important fact in this section is the structural symmetry between the founding trauma (mother dies, child survives, father blames) and the adult trauma (cousin dies, she survives, the death was at an ally's hand). The same shape twice. Her post-CWII collapse and recovery should be read as, among other things, a second chance to metabolize the first loss — which she did not have the developmental equipment for the first time. That she did the work the second time, on the page, in therapy, with witnesses, is the single most important piece of evidence in the file that she is not her father, not Brian Banner, not the cousin who could not integrate. She is the one who chose, alone among the gamma-touched and the Walters-named, to do the grief.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# UATU ANALYSIS FILE: WALTERS, JENNIFER SUSAN
## Section 7 — Relationships II: Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

The subject's chosen network is denser, older, and more load-bearing than her romantic register and substantially more stable than her family-of-origin file. The pattern across forty years of behavioral data: she selects relationships in which (a) she does not have to perform a single form, (b) the other party has independently survived something disfiguring (literal or metaphoric) and metabolized it, and (c) reciprocity is built into the structure of contact (legal practice, team service, neighborly proximity) so she is never alone with the question of whether to call. She is a profoundly skilled friend — generous, prompt, funny, willing to litigate on a friend's behalf at three in the morning — and a structurally avoidant one: the friendships that have lasted are the friendships where the other person reaches first when she goes silent. The following sub-sections map the persistent ties, ranked by load-bearing weight rather than chronology.

---

### 7.1 — Bruce Banner (cousin / ego-ideal / gamma-mirror / unresolved grief object)

The single most important relationship in the file, including romantic. He is the relational organ around which her entire adult identity is structured, and his death (Civil War II) is the wound she is still healing from in present-day continuity.

**History.** Summer-visit cousin during the period after Elaine Walters' death; described in flashback across multiple runs as the one adult in the extended family who spoke to her as a person and not as a problem. The relevant childhood image — recurrent across writers — is Bruce sitting with the small bookish Jennifer at a kitchen table, taking her questions seriously. He is the prototype of the Seen-and-Chosen experience she has been trying to reconstruct ever since. The transfusion (Savage She-Hulk #1) is therefore not just a medical event; it is a literalization of the pre-existing psychological fact that she is made partly out of him. She carries his blood. She carries his condition. She does not carry his curse — and this asymmetry is the second great organizing fact of the relationship.

**Dynamics.** Cousin-as-older-brother in affect, peer-as-fellow-monster in adulthood. She is the only person in his life who can talk to him as family without fear (she can take a punch) and without pity (she has the same etiology). Reciprocally, he is the only person who knows the gamma-experience from the inside, which means he is the only person who cannot be impressed or frightened by her form — the one social context in which she does not have to manage the impression she is making. With Bruce, both forms read as just-Jen. This is rare in the file; it may be unique in it.

**Evolution.** Three phases. (i) Pre-transfusion: tutelary, gentle. (ii) 1980s–2000s: peer, with intermittent friction during Banner's monstrous periods (she fights him at multiple points: Las Vegas, WWH). The WWH defeat is significant — the ego-ideal becomes the thing-that-can-destroy-her, and she has to revise the internal image without rejecting it. (iii) Civil War II: he is killed by Hawkeye while she is concurrently in cardiac arrest. She did not see it; she learned it in the hospital. This was foreclosure of repair on a relationship that was, at his death, mid-quarrel about how he was managing his condition. Survivor guilt is acute and well-documented in the Tamaki run.

**Trust/safety.** Total. She has lent him her body's last reserves; he has lent her his blood. Neither was ever priced.

**Failure mode.** When he was Hulk-dominant and dangerous, she defaulted to law-enforcement framing — bring him in, contain him — and was unable to access the cousin-frame in those windows. She has reproached herself for this. The reverse failure: when she was destabilized post-transformation in early continuity, he was not available to help integrate it because he was actively suffering his own condition. They were never well at the same time for long.

**Leverage (operational).** Invocation of his name is a live wire. Confronting her with the manner of his death, with Hawkeye's name, or with a counterfactual ("if you had been there") will reliably break composure. Conversely, anyone who genuinely understood Bruce can earn a startling amount of access very quickly. She has been known to extend professional and personal credit to people simply for having mourned him well.

---

### 7.2 — Patricia "Patsy" Walker / Hellcat (longest-running female friendship; chosen sister)

**History.** Friendship cemented during Avengers/West Coast tenure and Slott-era social orbit; sustained continuously across every subsequent run, including the Kate Leth Patsy Walker, A.K.A. Hellcat! era in which Jen functions as Patsy's lawyer, landlord-adjacent confidante, and adult-in-the-room.

**Dynamics.** Asymmetric in surface (Jen as the more financially stable, professionally credentialed, emotionally regulated of the pair) but symmetric underneath — both women carry maternal-loss material (Patsy's mother monstrously so), both have public personas that occlude private fragility, both have been resurrected from death in their respective files. Patsy is the friend who calls when she shouldn't, asks for too much, and gets it. Jen is the friend who shows up with a contract, a couch, and a sandwich. The reciprocal gift: Patsy is one of the very few people who can make Jen laugh hard enough to bypass her courtroom-in-the-head, and she does not let Jen disappear into work.

**Evolution.** Began as superhero-peer collegiality; deepened through shared experience of romantic catastrophe (both have annulled marriages on file, both have processed reproductive grief, both have been written by writers willing to let them have therapy on-page). In the Tamaki run Patsy is among the first to physically penetrate Jen's apartment isolation — uninvited, with food, refusing to leave. This is the friend-keeping behavior the file requires of anyone who wants to remain in Jen's life.

**Trust/safety.** Very high. Sub-Bruce, peer with Louise. Jen has discussed her father with Patsy — a small list.

**Failure mode.** Patsy's chaos absorbs more of Jen's regulation than the friendship technically should; in stressed periods Jen has used Patsy's larger problems as an excuse not to address her own (a documented intellectualization pattern — *I'm fine, look at her*). Patsy occasionally registers this and pushes back; the friendship survives the pushback every time.

**Leverage.** Threat or harm to Patsy will pull Jen out of any other commitment, including paid trial work, within hours. Conversely, asking Jen to choose between Patsy and an institution will reliably surface her institution-skeptical side.

---

### 7.3 — Louise "Weezi" Mason / The Blonde Phantom (mentor; older-woman archetype; surrogate mother-figure)

**History.** Introduced as Jen's paralegal/legal secretary in the Slott GLK&H run; canonically a Golden Age heroine pulled forward through narrative metaphysics, structurally an older woman with a complete life behind her who has chosen to spend her remaining time at Jen's side. The single most maternally-coded relationship in the entire file.

**Dynamics.** Louise mothers without infantilizing. She is the person who tells Jen when her hair looks tired, when her dress is wrong for the judge, when she's drinking too much, when she's avoiding John Jameson's calls — and Jen accepts the corrections, which she accepts from almost no one else. The relationship is one of the cleanest pieces of evidence that Jen's avoidance of intimacy is not characterological but specifically attachment-injury-shaped: with a safe, non-romantic, non-paternal older female figure, she is observably comfortable being seen, corrected, fussed over.

**Evolution.** Began transactional (employer/employee with metafictional twist), deepened into co-conspirator (Louise as career architect — she effectively engineered Jen's superhuman-law specialization), settled into the closest thing Jen has to a stable domestic-affection bond. Survives Jen's various moves, firings, and transformations. Louise's age and the unstable metaphysics of her continued existence are a quiet ambient grief Jen does not discuss.

**Trust/safety.** Highest tier. Jen will tell Louise things she will not tell Patsy because Louise will not be wounded by them — the older woman's emotional reserve creates safety that peer-friendship cannot.

**Failure mode.** Jen sometimes treats Louise as the mother she did not get and then resents Louise for not being able to deliver mother-things she did not actually ask for. These episodes are short; Louise is unrufflable.

**Leverage.** Any threat to Louise activates Jen's protective instinct with a ferocity that exceeds even the Patsy threshold, because Louise is irreplaceable in the role she occupies — losing her would re-open the maternal wound at its source.

---

### 7.4 — Awesome Andy / Andrew the Android (protégé; ward; ethics-laboratory)

**History.** GLK&H's android paralegal, originally a Mad Thinker construct; Jen took on his legal personhood case, won it, and then took on his moral education by default.

**Dynamics.** The clearest expression of Jen's mentor capacity. With Andy she is patient, didactic without condescension, and visibly proud of incremental progress. The relationship is also useful diagnostically: she relaxes around Andy in a way she does not relax around anyone with a normal social-evaluative apparatus, because Andy cannot judge her aesthetically. Some of her most uninhibited self-disclosure on-page is to Andy, who registers it as data and does not weaponize it.

**Evolution.** Client → colleague → friend → quasi-ward. When Andy's developmental crises (the absorbed-emotions arc) destabilized him, Jen led the response.

**Trust/safety.** High in the specific axis of being-not-judged-for-appearance; lower in the axis of practical emotional support, because Andy's reciprocity is structurally limited.

**Failure mode.** She occasionally over-attributes interiority to him and is then bruised when he behaves more mechanistically than her projection allowed. She corrects fast.

**Leverage.** Threat to Andy's legal personhood — any test case that would re-litigate it — will recruit her pro bono indefinitely.

---

### 7.5 — Mallory Book (rival turned colleague; mirror-from-the-other-side)

**History.** GLK&H colleague, initially Jen's adversary in firm politics and courtroom rivalry; over the Slott run becomes a working partner and eventually a friend.

**Dynamics.** Mallory is what Jen would have been if Jen had grown up beautiful, confident, and unloved-in-a-different-way: the polish without the gamma, the courtroom edge without the wit-as-defense. The friendship that emerges is hard-won, mediated through mutual professional respect, and one of the few in the file where Jen tolerates being the less-formidable presence in the room. This is developmentally useful for her — Mallory will not let her hide behind She-Hulk swagger; she demands Jennifer-the-attorney.

**Trust/safety.** Moderate-high; bounded by professional context. They are not late-night-phone-call friends; they are I-need-your-second-chair-tomorrow friends, which is a category Jen also values.

**Failure mode.** Competition can resurface under acute professional stress. They have learned to name it quickly.

**Leverage.** Professional humiliation of Mallory will activate Jen's loyalty; sincere praise of Mallory's work to Jen will produce an unguarded warmth that very few work-context interactions elicit.

---

### 7.6 — Holden Holliway (employer; father-surrogate, partially functional)

**History.** Senior partner who hired Jen at GLK&H on the condition she practice as Jennifer, not She-Hulk — the originating injury of her courtroom-dysphoria arc, but also a man who saw something in her worth conditioning a firm hire upon.

**Dynamics.** Authority figure who is exacting, withholding of warmth, occasionally cruel in his demands — and yet, unlike her actual father, ultimately invested in her flourishing on terms she can recognize. The transference is obvious and Jen knows it; she has joked about it. The fact that she stayed at the firm despite the dysphoric requirement is itself diagnostic: she will tolerate a great deal from a paternal figure who pays attention.

**Trust/safety.** Bounded. Professional trust high; personal trust low.

**Failure mode.** She over-performs for him in ways that replicate her childhood adaptation. Holliway, to his credit, mostly does not exploit it; when he has, the relationship has frayed and repaired.

**Leverage.** His approval is disproportionately motivating. His disapproval triggers regression more reliably than is healthy.

---

### 7.7 — Janet Van Dyne / The Wasp (Avengers-era mentor; femininity-as-power model)

**History.** Avengers tenure under Stern; Janet was chairperson during part of Jen's membership and the senior woman on the team.

**Dynamics.** Janet modeled for Jen a version of being a powerful woman in a male-dominated heroic context that did not require either de-sexing or being-only-sexual — the both/and that Jen was, at that point, still learning. Less close than Patsy or Louise, more formative. Jen's sense of how to dress for an Avengers function, how to handle press, how to take a meeting with a board, draws from observed Janet behavior in this window.

**Trust/safety.** Friendly across decades; not confidential.

**Failure mode.** Distance; they do not work to maintain the tie, and gaps of years are not uncommon.

**Leverage.** Janet's call gets answered. An Avengers-business request routed through Janet has different valence than one routed through Steve or Tony.

---

### 7.8 — Carol Danvers (peer; sparring partner; sometimes-strained ally)

**History.** Peer across multiple eras; teammates intermittently; occasional public-policy alignment (Civil War II found them on the same side, which was itself part of how Jen got into the Thanos fight that wounded her).

**Dynamics.** Mutual respect, real warmth, structural friction. Both women are accustomed to being the most-formidable person in the room; the relationship works best in operational contexts and gets thornier in social ones. Carol is one of very few people who can physically restrain Jen if needed, which is a quiet fact both of them know and do not discuss.

**Trust/safety.** High in crisis, moderate in peacetime.

**Failure mode.** Civil War II is the open wound. Jen has not formally blamed Carol for the cascade that killed Bruce and nearly killed her, but the relationship has carried that weight since, and Tamaki-era beats indicate Jen has not fully forgiven and is not pretending to have.

**Leverage.** Direct invocation of Civil War II will produce either icy lawyering or controlled rage; there is no neutral response available.

---

### 7.9 — Reed and Sue Richards (Fantastic Four era; complicated extended-family)

**History.** Replaced the Thing on the FF roster (Byrne run), lived in the Baxter Building, was treated as family by Sue and tolerated-with-affection by Reed.

**Dynamics.** Sue provided a second model (with Janet) of competent adult womanhood — specifically a model of being a mother and a hero, which Jen has filed away without using. Reed is the figure who later stripped her of powers without consent, a betrayal she has named as such; the relationship survived but is permanently asterisked.

**Trust/safety.** Sue: high, durable. Reed: contingent, conditional, courteous.

**Failure mode.** The power-stripping incident is the precedent that any attempt to alter her body without consent is unforgivable. Reed walked this back; Jen accepted the walk-back; the file remains.

**Leverage.** Sue's intervention can de-escalate Jen in contexts where peer intervention cannot. Reed cannot ask anything that requires bodily compliance.

---

### 7.10 — Hank Pym, Tony Stark, Steve Rogers (institutional-elder cohort; differentiated)

Grouped because the dynamics are structurally similar — older male hero-statesmen whose approval she registers, whose flaws she litigates internally, and with whom she maintains professional warmth without confessional intimacy.

- **Steve Rogers:** Despite having prosecuted him in Civil War, the relationship survived because both of them treated it as institutional rather than personal. She respects him; he respects that she did her job. This is the cleanest example in the file of her capacity to separate role from person.
- **Tony Stark:** Warmth, mutual wit, structural wariness. Tony is the figure most likely to ask her to do something her bar would not love; she has said no, and the relationship survives the no.
- **Hank Pym:** Cordial; she has worked his legal matters; she does not love his marital file.

**Leverage across all three:** her cooperation can be requested but not assumed; she will not be a tame Avengers lawyer for any of them.

---

### 7.11 — Jazinda the Skrull (chosen-family / sister-by-circumstance / road-period co-pilot)

**History.** Peter David run; bounty-hunting partnership across the country during a fugitive period; cohabitation in a trailer.

**Dynamics.** The most domestic relationship in the file outside of romance. Jen and Jazinda kept house, fought together, raised the question of family between them in ways the canon left deliberately ambiguous. Read as platonic chosen-sister in the present file. Jazinda is the figure with whom Jen lived in closest sustained physical proximity, longer than any romantic partner.

**Evolution.** Began transactional (Jen was hired); deepened rapidly into protectiveness; ended in geographic separation that has not been satisfactorily closed in canon.

**Trust/safety.** Very high for the duration of the partnership; somewhat in abeyance since.

**Failure mode.** Jen left when the practice called her back. She has not fully reckoned with what Jazinda needed from her after.

**Leverage.** Jazinda's safety would pull her across continents without consultation.

---

### 7.12 — Hellcat-adjacent and post-Tamaki support network (the therapy era cohort)

The Tamaki run introduced a quieter, less-superpowered ring of contacts who carried her through the post-CWII recovery: her doormen, a neighbor, a regular café staffer, the therapist (on-page, named in continuity), and Bradley, the office assistant whose ordinariness she found restorative. These are not flashy relationships; they are diagnostically critical. After the trauma she rebuilt her social ecology from civilian-tier upward, starting with people who had no idea what She-Hulk had done last week and no interest in finding out. The therapist relationship in particular is the first clearly-rendered professional-help engagement in her file and represents a developmental milestone — she finally consented to be a patient.

**Trust/safety.** Variable but real; the therapist relationship is unusually disclosing for her.

**Failure mode.** She has historically dropped these ties when the gamma-tier life resumes; the present-continuity test is whether she will maintain them now.

**Leverage.** Anyone who knew her in the apartment-period has access to a Jen who has not been visible to most of her superhuman cohort.

---

### 7.13 — Junior associates and protégés (cumulative pattern)

Across runs, Jen has consistently taken junior attorneys, paralegals, interns, and young heroes seriously in a way her seniors did not always take her. She remembers names. She writes recommendations. She returns calls from people who can do nothing for her. This is one of the cleanest pieces of evidence for the prosocial-value-system entry in §5 and one of the structural reasons her professional network is denser than her romantic one. She is, on the evidence, building the mentor she did not have, repeatedly, by being it.

**Failure mode.** She occasionally over-identifies with a struggling junior and absorbs their crisis as her own; she is learning, slowly, to refer out.

**Leverage.** Any former protégé in distress will pull her in.

---

### 7.14 — Summary architecture of the chosen network

Three concentric rings.

- **Inner ring (load-bearing, irreplaceable):** Bruce (deceased, unresolved), Louise, Patsy. The people in front of whom both forms read as just-Jen.
- **Middle ring (durable, contextual):** Jazinda, Mallory, Andy, Sue Richards, the therapist, Janet. The people who hold specific axes of her life.
- **Outer ring (institutional, cooperative):** Carol, Steve, Tony, Reed, Hank, Holliway, the wider Avengers/FF/bar apparatus. Professional warmth, real respect, bounded intimacy.

The structural observation: the inner ring is small, all-female (Bruce excepted, and he is dead), and selected over decades for the specific capacity to reach toward her when she withdraws. The middle and outer rings are large, mixed, and selected for shared mission. There is no domestic-romantic figure in any ring — the romantic file is structurally separate from the chosen-family file, and this separation is itself the diagnostic finding. She has built a family. She has not yet built a household. The two projects are different, and only one of them is, on present evidence, complete.


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# UATU ANALYSIS FILE: WALTERS, JENNIFER SUSAN
## Section 7 — Relationships III: Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

Preliminary note. The taxonomy in this section is operational, not moral. "Rival" denotes a sustained adversarial-but-legitimate counterparty (opposing counsel, ideological opponent, competitor for status or jurisdiction). "Enemy" denotes a party who has tried, materially, to harm her or her clients. "Lover" denotes documented sexual/romantic intimacy of more than incidental duration. There is overlap — at least three subjects appear in more than one category — and the overlaps themselves are diagnostic. Walters does not, as a rule, hate the people she sleeps with, but she does sleep with people who scare her in legible ways. The pattern is too consistent across forty years of behavior to be coincidence.

---

### I. RIVALS

#### Mallory Book

The most psychologically important non-romantic relationship in Walters's adult professional life. Book — senior partner at Goodman, Lieber, Kurtzberg & Holliway during Walters's tenure there — is the woman Walters might have been if Walters had never been shot: tall, brunette, conventionally beautiful, courtroom-lethal, contemptuous of superhuman exceptionalism, and operating in human form by choice and on merit. Book treats Walters as a lightweight who got her firm seat because of her green form, not despite it. This is precisely the wound: Book sees the She-Hulk as a credential rather than a body, and she sees Jennifer-the-lawyer as derivative of it.

*Dynamic:* Hostile mentorship masquerading as collegial rivalry. Book sets harder cases for Walters than for other associates. Walters responds by over-preparing, which is what Book intended. Surveillance behavior consistent with this: Walters reads every brief Book files, keeps a physical folder of Book's appellate work, and when stressed will mutter Book's name under her breath during her own prep sessions — a courtroom-in-the-head cast member.

*Trust/safety:* Low trust, high predictability. Book will not lie to her, will not stab her in the back procedurally, and will demolish her on the merits in open court without apology. This is, perversely, a safer object relation than most of her father-coded male authority figures, because the cruelty is bounded by rules Walters herself believes in.

*Failure mode:* When Book targets the human form specifically — implies Walters is "playing dress-up" in either body — Walters's affect tightens, her wit goes brittle, and she over-litigates. The schema-touch is the small-girl schema.

*Leverage:* For Walters over Book — pro bono and superhuman-context cases where Book's human-law instincts misread the room. For Book over Walters — any framing that makes the green form a costume rather than a self.

#### Titania (Mary MacPherran)

The longest-running interpersonal rivalry in the file, spanning from Secret Wars (1984) through every era since. Titania was made — Doom's gift, a small angry woman given a large angry body — and the parallel to Walters is too exact for either of them to ignore. Both are women whose powers solved their adolescent humiliation; Titania metabolized hers as resentment and crime, Walters as confidence and law. They are the alternate-self each could have become.

*Dynamic:* Walters wins most of the physical contests, especially after the first humiliating Secret Wars defeat. Titania has, on multiple documented occasions, ambushed Walters specifically — choosing her, traveling to her, picking the fight. This is not professional villainy; it is personal. Walters, for her part, takes Titania more seriously than other recurring brawlers of comparable power: she remembers the names of Titania's relatives, asks after her in opposing-counsel small talk during Frank Payne / Crusher Creel cases, and has on at least one occasion refused to press charges where she could have.

*Evolution:* Genuine, slow, partial mutual recognition. The two have shared a drink. They will never be friends. The hostility has converted from terror (Walters, post first defeat) to a stable, almost ritual antagonism.

*Trust/safety:* Predictable hostility, no surprises. Walters will not be poisoned or sandbagged by Titania; she will be punched, on schedule, in a parking lot.

*Failure mode:* Titania goes for the human form. Has done so on at least two documented occasions — attacking Walters while she was de-transformed, which is the move that Walters experiences as obscene rather than merely violent. The trespass is across the somatic-psychological boundary in §2.

*Leverage:* Titania's leverage on Walters is the small-girl schema reflected back. Walters's leverage on Titania is the same in reverse — Titania knows Walters knows.

#### Pug (Augustus Pugliese) — rival-adjacent

Not a true rival but worth noting in this section because the relational geometry is competitive-romantic. Pug was Walters's co-associate at GLK&H, in love with her, friend-zoned with full awareness on her part, and resentful of every man she dated who was either larger than him or more famous than him (i.e., all of them). Walters's behavior toward Pug is a near-perfect demonstration of the avoidant-intimacy pattern: she accepts his devotion as ambient infrastructure, declines to reciprocate it, and is genuinely bewildered when he eventually exits in pain. The pattern repeats with other "safe" men across her life. She does not see them as romantic options because they do not activate the threat-response she has learned to recognize as desire.

---

### II. ENEMIES

#### Nicholas Trask

The origin enemy. LA-based crime boss whose gunmen shot Walters and thereby produced the transfusion event. Trask spent the early Savage She-Hulk run trying to finish what his men started and to suppress what she had become.

*Dynamic:* Walters hunted him with a focus and physical pleasure that would, in a less psychologically literate file, raise concern. The Trask arc is where she first discovered that she liked being feared. Trask is the man who made her, in two senses — proximate cause of the GSW, proximate cause of her becoming.

*Closure:* Trask was neutralized in canon decades ago. He persists in the file as a structuring memory: the first object on whom she practiced agency.

*Leverage:* None active. Symbolic only.

#### The Leader (Samuel Sterns)

Gamma-cohort antagonist, recurrent across her Avengers and solo tenure. The Leader treats Walters as a specimen and a chess piece — gamma family, therefore family business. The relationship is contaminated by his pre-existing antagonism to Bruce: she is the cousin, and he addresses her as such.

*Dynamic:* Patronizing condescension on his part; cold, sharp counter-strategy on hers. Walters has on at least one documented occasion outmaneuvered the Leader by reading him as opposing counsel rather than as a supervillain — i.e., constructing his case for him and pre-empting it. Effective. He does not expect to be lawyered.

*Failure mode:* He invokes Bruce. Post-Civil War II, this is a live-wire. Pre-CWII she could parry it; post-CWII it can stagger her mid-confrontation.

*Leverage:* He has, at various points, claimed knowledge of gamma physiology that he might use to threaten or alter her form. The threat-to-bodily-agency vector is the most operationally dangerous one against her (cf. §5 pressure points), and the Leader is one of two parties on the planet positioned to exploit it.

#### Abomination (Emil Blonsky)

The cousin's enemy, inherited. Blonsky's significance in Walters's file is less personal than archetypal: he is what Bruce feared becoming and what Walters proved gamma transformation does not have to become. Direct conflict between them has been intermittent and physical. She has beaten him; she has been beaten by him; the engagements lack the personal grain of her Titania fights or her Leader chess.

*Leverage:* Symbolic — he is, in her internal map, the proof that her cousin's terror was rational.

#### The Wrecking Crew, the Circus of Crime, the standard rogues' bench

Walters has a working enemy class of mid-tier physical antagonists she does not take personally and they do not take personally back. Her file notes here are operational: she fights cleanly, prefers to neutralize rather than maim, takes statements afterward, and has on multiple occasions represented members of this class in subsequent unrelated matters. The professional/personal partition is real and is one of her sturdier ethical structures.

#### Hawkeye (Clint Barton) — enemy-adjacent, by event

Not classed as enemy by Walters in conscious narrative, but the file must register it: Barton killed Bruce. The act was the precipitating loss of her CWII trauma. Walters prosecuted him. She conducted herself professionally. She lost. The relational consequence is permanent, asymmetric, unspoken: she will be in a room with him, she will not be alone in a room with him, she will not work a case with him by choice, and if pressed she will name the avoidance as professional rather than personal. This is intellectualization (§3) operating in real time.

*Failure mode:* Any social configuration that forces extended proximity. Avengers reunions are managed; she arrives late, leaves early, positions herself across the room.

*Leverage:* None either way. The grief is the leverage and it lives in her, not between them.

#### Titania's husband (Crusher Creel, the Absorbing Man)

Covered under Titania by association. Walters treats Creel as the spouse of her rival — politely, with low-grade professional courtesy when their legal paths cross (and they have, repeatedly; she has appeared adjacent to Creel in superhuman criminal matters multiple times). She does not hate him. He does not hate her. They are connected by the woman they both, in different ways, respect.

#### Her father — internal enemy

William Morris Walters is treated structurally in earlier sections. He belongs in this section too, because he is the most operationally relevant antagonist she has ever had. He is the only person whose disapproval can fully collapse her adult competence into the small-girl schema in under thirty seconds. He has done so on documented occasions across every decade of the file. He has never tried to kill her. He has done worse: he has refused to see her.

*Leverage:* Total, in the affective dimension; null in the physical. Operationally, any actor seeking to destabilize Walters quickly should look here first.

---

### III. LOVERS

The romantic file is long, the partners are heterogeneous, and the pattern is uniform. With the exception of one or two outliers discussed below, every documented partner is either (a) physically her equal or superior, eliminating the vulnerability differential she finds intolerable, or (b) emotionally unavailable in a way that mirrors her father, eliminating the intimacy threshold she finds intolerable. Many partners are both. The relationships run hot, often spectacularly so, and end at the point where sustained domestic mutuality would begin.

#### Zapper (Daniel Ridge)

First documented adult partner, pre-transformation period and continuing briefly post-. Medical student, conventionally good-looking, devoted, ordinary. The "safe" man. The relationship did not survive her transformation, ostensibly because of the danger, actually because she ceased to need what he offered. Zapper is the control variable in the experiment: the kind of man she chose before she had options. She has not chosen one like him since.

*Failure mode:* Mutual outgrowth. Closure clean.

#### Wyatt Wingfoot

The longest-running on-and-off significant partnership in the file.
